Historically, some corporations were named after the members of their boards of directors: for example, the "<a href="/wiki/President_and_Fellows_of_Harvard_College" title="President and Fellows of Harvard College">President and Fellows of Harvard College</a>
                                        " is the name of one of the two governing boards of <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard University</a>
                                        , but it is also the exact name under which Harvard was legally incorporated.
                                        <sup id="cite_ref-GoverningHarvard_35-0" class="reference">
                                            <a href="#cite_note-GoverningHarvard-35">&#91;34 &#93;</a>
                                        </sup>
                                        Nowadays, corporations in most jurisdictions may have a distinct name that does not need to make reference to the members of their boards. In Canada, this possibility is taken to its logical extreme: many smaller Canadian corporations have no names at all, merely numbers based on a registration number (for example, "12345678 Ontario Limited"), which is assigned by the provincial or territorial government where the corporation incorporates.

                                    </p>
                                    <p>
                                        In most countries, corporate names include a term or an abbreviation that denotes the corporate status of the entity (for example, "Incorporated" or "Inc." in the United States) or the limited liability of its members (for example, "Limited", "Ltd.", or "LLC").
                                        <sup id="cite_ref-Bartlett_Page54_36-0" class="reference">
                                            <a href="#cite_note-Bartlett_Page54-36">&#91;35 &#93;</a>
                                        </sup>
                                        These terms vary by jurisdiction and language. In some jurisdictions, they are mandatory, and in others, such as California, they are not.
                                        <sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference">
                                            <a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;36 &#93;</a>
                                        </sup>
                                        Their use puts everybody on <a href="/wiki/Constructive_notice" title="Constructive notice">constructive notice</a>
                                        that they are dealing with an entity whose <a href="/wiki/Legal_liability" title="Legal liability">liability</a>
                                        is limited: one can only collect from whatever assets the entity still controls when one obtains a judgment against it.

                                    </p>
                                    <p>
                                        Some jurisdictions do not allow the use of the word "<b>company</b>
                                        " alone to denote corporate status, since the word "<a href="/wiki/Company" title="Company">company</a>
                                        " may refer to a partnership or some other form of collective ownership (in the United States it can be used by a <a href="/wiki/Sole_proprietorship" title="Sole proprietorship">sole proprietorship</a>
                                        but this is not generally the case elsewhere).
                                        <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
                                            &#91;
                                            <i>
                                                <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
                                                    <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2012)">citation needed</span>
                                                </a>
                                            </i>
                                            &#93;
                                        </sup>
                                    </p>
                                    <h2>
                                        <span class="mw-headline" id="Personhood">Personhood</span>
                                        <span class="mw-editsection">
                                            <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span>
                                            <a href="/w/index.php?title=Corporation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Personhood">
                                                <span>edit</span>
                                            </a>
                                            <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span>
                                        </span>
                                    </h2>
                                    <p>
                                        Despite not being human beings, corporations have been ruled <a href="/wiki/Legal_personality" class="mw-redirect" title="Legal personality">legal persons</a>
                                        in a few countries, and have many of the same rights as  <a href="/wiki/Natural_person" title="Natural person">natural persons</a>
                                        do. For example, a corporation can own property, and can sue or be sued for as long as it exists. Corporations can exercise <a href="/wiki/Human_rights" title="Human rights">human rights</a>
                                        against real individuals and the state,
                                        <sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference">
                                            <a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;37 &#93;</a>
                                        </sup>
                                        <sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference">
                                            <a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;38 &#93;</a>
                                        </sup>
                                        and they can themselves be responsible for human rights violations.
                                        <sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference">
                                            <a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;39 &#93;</a>
                                        </sup>
                                        Corporations can be "dissolved" either by statutory operation, the order of the court, or voluntary action on the part of shareholders. <a href="/wiki/Insolvency" title="Insolvency">Insolvency</a>
                                        may result in a form of corporate failure, when creditors force the liquidation and dissolution of the corporation under court order,
                                        <sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference">
                                            <a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;40 &#93;</a>
                                        </sup>
                                        but it most often results in a restructuring of corporate holdings. Corporations can even be convicted of special criminal offenses in the UK, such as <a href="/wiki/Fraud" title="Fraud">fraud</a>
                                        and <a href="/wiki/Corporate_manslaughter" title="Corporate manslaughter">corporate manslaughter</a>
                                        . However, corporations are not considered living entities in the way that humans are.
                                        <sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference">
                                            <a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;41 &#93;</a>
                                        </sup>
                                    </p>
                                    <p>
                                        Legal scholars and others, such as <a href="/wiki/Joel_Bakan" title="Joel Bakan">Joel Bakan</a>
                                        , have observed that a business corporation created as a "legal person" has a <a href="/wiki/Psychopathy" title="Psychopathy">psychopathic personality</a>
                                        because it is required to elevate its own interests above those of others even when this <a href="/wiki/Externality" title="Externality">inflicts major risks and grave harms</a>
                                        on the public or on other third-parties. Such critics note that the legal mandate of the corporation to focus exclusively on corporate profits and self-interest often victimizes employees, customers, the public at large, and/or the <a href="/wiki/Environmental_economics" title="Environmental economics">natural resources</a>
                                        .
                                        <sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference">
                                            <a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;42 &#93;</a>
                                        </sup>
                                        The political theorist <a href="/wiki/David_Runciman" title="David Runciman">David Runciman</a>
                                        notes that corporate personhood forms a fundamental part of the modern
                                        <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
                                            &#91;
                                            <i>
                                                <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers">
                                                    <span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (February 2022)">when?</span>
                                                </a>
                                            </i>
                                            &#93;
                                        </sup>
                                        history of the idea of the <a href="/wiki/State_(polity)" title="State (polity)">state</a>
                                        , and believes the idea of the corporation as legal persons can help to clarify the role of citizens as political <a href="/wiki/Stakeholder_(corporate)" title="Stakeholder (corporate)">stakeholders</a>
                                        , and to break down the sharp conceptual dichotomy between the state and the people or the individual, a distinction that, on his account, is "increasingly unable to meet the demands placed on the state in the modern world".
                                        <sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference">
                                            <a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;43 &#93;</a>
                                        </sup>
                                    </p>

                                    <div class="reflist reflist-lower-alpha">
                                        <div class="mw-references-wrap">
                                            <ol class="references">
                                                <li id="cite_note-34">
                                                    <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
                                                        <b>
                                                            <a href="#cite_ref-34">^</a>
                                                        </b>
                                                    </span>
                                                    <span class="reference-text">
                                                        Aside from the infeasibility of the ableness of one corporation to supply an entire market demand, there are a number of national as well as international <a href="/wiki/Competition_law" title="Competition law">competition laws</a>
                                                        regulating antitrust behaviour that are intended to restrict this type of activity by instead favouring the differentiation of markets.
                                                        <sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference">
                                                            <a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33 &#93;</a>
                                                        </sup>
                                                    </span>
                                                </li>

                                                    <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
                                                        <b>
                                                            <a href="#cite_ref-Bartlett_Page54_36-0">^</a>
                                                        </b>
                                                    </span>
                                                    <span class="reference-text">
                                                        <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1133582631">
                                                        <cite id="CITEREFBartlett1995" class="citation book cs1">
                                                            Bartlett, Joseph W. (1995). 
                                                            <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=j5ngCEGebrwC&amp;pg=PA54">
                                                                <i>Equity Finance: Venture Capital, Buyouts, Restructurings and Reorganizations</i>
                                                            </a>
                                                            (2nd &#160;ed.). New York: Aspen Publishers. p.&#160;54. <a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>
                                                            &#160;
                                                            <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-07355-7077-1" title="Special:BookSources/978-07355-7077-1">

                                                        <span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Equity+Finance%3A+Venture+Capital%2C+Buyouts%2C+Restructurings+and+Reorganizations&amp;rft.place=New+York&amp;rft.pages=54&amp;rft.edition=2nd&amp;rft.pub=Aspen+Publishers&amp;rft.date=1995&amp;rft.isbn=978-07355-7077-1&amp;rft.aulast=Bartlett&amp;rft.aufirst=Joseph+W.&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3Dj5ngCEGebrwC%26pg%3DPA54&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ACorporation" class="Z3988"></span>

                                                </li>
                                                <li id="cite_note-37">
                                                    <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
                                                        <b>
                                                            <a href="#cite_ref-37">^</a>
                                                        </b>
                                                    </span>
                                                    <span class="reference-text">
                                                        California does not require corporations to indicate corporate status in their names, except for close corporations. The drafters of the 1977 revision of the California General Corporation Law considered the possibility of forcing all California corporations to have a name indicating corporate status, but decided against it because of the huge number of corporations that would have had to change their names, and the lack of any evidence that anyone had been harmed in California by entities whose corporate status was not immediately apparent from their names. However, the 1977 drafters were able to impose the current disclosure requirement for close corporations. See Harold Marsh, Jr., R. Roy Finkle, Larry W. Sonsini, and Ann Yvonne Walker, 
                                                        <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=uVKArsf8CtIC&amp;pg=SA5-PA15">
                                                            <i>Marsh's California Corporation Law</i>
                                                        </a>
                                                        , 4th ed., vol. 1 (New York: Aspen Publishers, 2004), 5â€“15 â€” 5â€“16.
                                                    </span>
                                                </li>
                                                <li id="cite_note-38">
                                                    <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
                                                        <b>
                                                            <a href="#cite_ref-38">^</a>
                                                        </b>

                                                            <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20120617104051/http://fds.oup.com/www.oup.co.uk/pdf/0-19-928983-2.pdf">

                                                            </a>
                                                            <span class="cs1-format">(PDF)</span>
